<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Solar eclipse</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
								<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Eclipse of the Sun" redirects here. For the film, see <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_of_the_Sun_(film)" title="Eclipse of the Sun (film)">Eclipse of the Sun (film)</a>. For the novel, see <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_of_the_Sun_(novel)" title="Eclipse of the Sun (novel)">Eclipse of the Sun (novel)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="width: 320px;">
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg" class="image" title="Total solar eclipse"><img alt="Total solar eclipse" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/308px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg" width="308" height="303" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/462px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/616px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3543" data-file-height="3489" /></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>A <i>total solar eclipse</i> occurs when the Moon completely covers the Sun's disk, as seen in this <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_August_11,_1999" title="Solar eclipse of August 11, 1999">1999 solar eclipse</a>. <a href="/wiki/Solar_prominence" title="Solar prominence">Solar prominences</a> can be seen along the limb (in red) as well as extensive <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">coronal</a> filaments.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:Annular_Eclipse._Taken_from_Middlegate,_Nevada_on_May_20,_2012.jpg" class="image" title="Annular solar eclipse"><img alt="Annular solar eclipse" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Annular_Eclipse._Taken_from_Middlegate%2C_Nevada_on_May_20%2C_2012.jpg/154px-Annular_Eclipse._Taken_from_Middlegate%2C_Nevada_on_May_20%2C_2012.jpg" width="154" height="154" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Annular_Eclipse._Taken_from_Middlegate%2C_Nevada_on_May_20%2C_2012.jpg/231px-Annular_Eclipse._Taken_from_Middlegate%2C_Nevada_on_May_20%2C_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Annular_Eclipse._Taken_from_Middlegate%2C_Nevada_on_May_20%2C_2012.jpg/308px-Annular_Eclipse._Taken_from_Middlegate%2C_Nevada_on_May_20%2C_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1801" data-file-height="1801" /></a><a href="/wiki/File:Partial_solar_eclipse_Oct_23_2014_Minneapolis_5-36pm_Ruen1.png" class="image" title="Partial solar eclipse"><img alt="Partial solar eclipse" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Partial_solar_eclipse_Oct_23_2014_Minneapolis_5-36pm_Ruen1.png/154px-Partial_solar_eclipse_Oct_23_2014_Minneapolis_5-36pm_Ruen1.png" width="154" height="154" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Partial_solar_eclipse_Oct_23_2014_Minneapolis_5-36pm_Ruen1.png/231px-Partial_solar_eclipse_Oct_23_2014_Minneapolis_5-36pm_Ruen1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Partial_solar_eclipse_Oct_23_2014_Minneapolis_5-36pm_Ruen1.png/308px-Partial_solar_eclipse_Oct_23_2014_Minneapolis_5-36pm_Ruen1.png 2x" data-file-width="1181" data-file-height="1180" /></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>An <i>annular solar eclipse</i> (left) occurs when the Moon is too far away to completely cover the Sun's disk (<a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_20,_2012" title="Solar eclipse of May 20, 2012">May 20, 2012</a>). During a <i>partial solar eclipse</i> (right), the Moon blocks only part of the Sun's disk (<a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_October_23,_2014" title="Solar eclipse of October 23, 2014">October 23, 2014</a>).</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>A <b>solar eclipse</b> (as seen from the planet <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>) is a type of <a href="/wiki/Eclipse" title="Eclipse">eclipse</a> that occurs when the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> passes between the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and Earth, and when the Moon fully or partially blocks ("<a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occults</a>") the Sun. This can happen only at <a href="/wiki/New_moon" title="New moon">new moon</a> when the Sun and the Moon are in <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">conjunction</a> as seen from Earth in an alignment referred to as <a href="/wiki/Syzygy_(astronomy)" title="Syzygy (astronomy)">syzygy</a>. In a total <a href="/wiki/Eclipse" title="Eclipse">eclipse</a>, the disk of the Sun is fully obscured by the Moon. In <a href="#Types">partial and annular eclipses</a>, only part of the Sun is obscured.</p>
<p>If the Moon were in a perfectly circular orbit, a little closer to the Earth, and in the same <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">orbital plane</a>, there would be total solar eclipses every new moon. However, since the Moon's orbit is <a href="/wiki/Inclination" class="mw-redirect" title="Inclination">tilted</a> at more than 5 degrees to the Earth's orbit around the Sun, its shadow usually misses Earth. The Moon's orbit must cross Earth's <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> plane in order for an eclipse (both solar as well as <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar</a>) to occur. In addition, the Moon's actual orbit is elliptical, often taking it far enough away from Earth that its <a href="/wiki/Apparent_size" class="mw-redirect" title="Apparent size">apparent size</a> is not large enough to block the Sun entirely. The orbital planes cross each other at a <a href="/wiki/Line_of_nodes" class="mw-redirect" title="Line of nodes">line of nodes</a> resulting in at least two, and up to five, solar eclipses occurring each year; no more than two of which can be total eclipses.<sup id="cite_ref-totality_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totality-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> However, total solar eclipses are rare at any particular location because totality exists only along a narrow path on the Earth's surface traced by the Moon's shadow or <a href="/wiki/Umbra" class="mw-redirect" title="Umbra">umbra</a>.</p>
<p>An eclipse is a <a href="/wiki/Natural_phenomenon" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural phenomenon">natural phenomenon</a>. However, in some ancient and modern cultures, solar eclipses were attributed to <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> causes or regarded as bad <a href="/wiki/Omen" title="Omen">omens</a>. A total solar eclipse can be frightening to people who are unaware of its <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomical</a> explanation, as the Sun seems to disappear during the day and the sky darkens in a matter of minutes.</p>
<p>Since <a href="/wiki/Sungazing" title="Sungazing">looking directly at the Sun</a> can lead to permanent eye damage or blindness, special eye protection or indirect viewing techniques are used when viewing a solar eclipse. It is technically safe to view only the total phase of a total solar eclipse with the unaided eye and without protection; however, this is a dangerous practice, as most people are not trained to recognize the phases of an eclipse, which can span over two hours while the total phase can only last a maximum of 7.5 minutes for any one location. People referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Eclipse_chasers" class="mw-redirect" title="Eclipse chasers">eclipse chasers</a></i> or <i>umbraphiles</i> will travel to remote locations to observe or witness predicted central solar eclipses.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparison_angular_diameter.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Comparison of minimum and maximum apparent sizes of the Sun and Moon (and planets). An annular eclipse can occur when the Sun has a larger apparent size than the Moon, whereas a total eclipse can occur when the Moon has a larger apparent size.</div>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg/220px-The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg/330px-The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg/440px-The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1497" data-file-height="1453" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Patrial_Eclipse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Partial solar eclipse during annular <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_20,_2012" title="Solar eclipse of May 20, 2012">solar eclipse of May 20, 2012</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are four types of solar eclipses:</p>
<ul>
<li>A <b>total eclipse</b> occurs when the dark silhouette of the Moon completely obscures the intensely bright light of the Sun, allowing the much fainter solar <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> to be visible. During any one eclipse, totality occurs at best only in a narrow track on the surface of Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> This narrow track is called the path of totality.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li>An <b>annular eclipse</b> occurs when the Sun and Moon are exactly in line with the Earth, but the apparent size of the Moon is smaller than that of the Sun. Hence the Sun appears as a very bright ring, or <a href="/wiki/Annulus_(mathematics)" title="Annulus (mathematics)">annulus</a>, surrounding the dark disk of the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington10_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington10-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <b>hybrid eclipse</b> (also called <b>annular/total eclipse</b>) shifts between a total and annular eclipse. At certain points on the surface of Earth, it appears as a total eclipse, whereas at other points it appears as annular. Hybrid eclipses are comparatively rare.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington10_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington10-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <b>partial eclipse</b> occurs when the Sun and Moon are not exactly in line with the Earth and the Moon only partially obscures the Sun. This phenomenon can usually be seen from a large part of the Earth outside of the track of an annular or total eclipse. However, some eclipses can only be seen as a partial eclipse, because the <a href="/wiki/Umbra" class="mw-redirect" title="Umbra">umbra</a> passes above the Earth's polar regions and never intersects the Earth's surface.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington10_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington10-7">[7]</a></sup> Partial eclipses are virtually unnoticeable in terms of the sun's brightness, as it takes well over 90% coverage to notice any darkening at all. Even at 99%, it would be no darker than <a href="/wiki/Civil_twilight" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil twilight">civil twilight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Of course, partial eclipses (and partial stages of other eclipses) can be observed if one is viewing the sun through a darkening filter (which should always be used for safety).</li>
</ul>
<p>The Sun's distance from Earth is about 400 times the Moon's distance, and the Sun's <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> is about 400 times the Moon's diameter. Because these ratios are approximately the same, the Sun and the Moon as seen from Earth appear to be approximately the same size: about 0.5 <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_arc" class="mw-redirect" title="Degree of arc">degree of arc</a> in angular measure.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington10_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington10-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>A separate category of solar eclipses is that of the Sun being occluded by a body other than the Earth's moon, as can be observed at points in space away from the Earth's surface. Two examples are when the crew of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a> observed the <a href="/wiki/File:Earth_Eclipses_Sun-ap12-s80-37406.jpg" title="File:Earth Eclipses Sun-ap12-s80-37406.jpg">Earth eclipse the Sun</a> in 1969 and when the <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">probe</a> observed <a href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg" title="File:Saturn eclipse.jpg">Saturn eclipsing the Sun</a> in 2006.</p>
<p>The Moon's orbit around the Earth is slightly <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">elliptical</a>, as is the Earth's orbit around the Sun. The apparent sizes of the Sun and Moon therefore vary.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_of_an_eclipse" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnitude of an eclipse">magnitude of an eclipse</a> is the ratio of the apparent size of the Moon to the apparent size of the Sun during an eclipse. An eclipse that occurs when the Moon is near its closest distance to Earth (<i>i.e.,</i> near its <a href="/wiki/Perigee" class="mw-redirect" title="Perigee">perigee</a>) can be a total eclipse because the Moon will appear to be large enough to completely cover the Sun's bright disk or <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">photosphere</a>; a total eclipse has a magnitude greater than or equal to 1.000. Conversely, an eclipse that occurs when the Moon is near its farthest distance from Earth (<i>i.e.,</i> near its <a href="/wiki/Apogee" class="mw-redirect" title="Apogee">apogee</a>) can only be an annular eclipse because the Moon will appear to be slightly smaller than the Sun; the magnitude of an annular eclipse is less than 1. Slightly more solar eclipses are annular than total because, on average, the Moon lies too far from Earth to cover the Sun completely.</p>
<p>A hybrid eclipse occurs when the magnitude of an eclipse changes during the event from less to greater than one, so the eclipse appears to be total at locations nearer the midpoint, and annular at other locations nearer the beginning and end, since the sides of the Earth are slightly further away from the Moon. These eclipses are extremely narrow in their path width and extremely short in their duration at any point, at most just a few seconds at any location within just a few kilometres of the centerline of the path. Like a <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focal point</a>, the width and duration of totality and annularity are near zero at the points where the changes between the two occur.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because the Earth's orbit around the Sun is also elliptical, the Earth's distance from the Sun similarly varies throughout the year. This affects the apparent size of the Sun in the same way, but not as much as does the Moon's varying distance from Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington10_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington10-7">[7]</a></sup> When Earth approaches its <a href="/wiki/Aphelion" class="mw-redirect" title="Aphelion">farthest distance from the Sun</a> in early July, a total eclipse is somewhat more likely, whereas conditions favour an annular eclipse when Earth approaches its <a href="/wiki/Perihelion" class="mw-redirect" title="Perihelion">closest distance to the Sun</a> in early January.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology_for_central_eclipse">Terminology for central eclipse</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg/220px-Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg/330px-Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg/440px-Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Each icon shows the view from the centre of its black spot, representing the moon (not to scale)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Central eclipse</i> is often used as a generic term for a total, annular, or hybrid eclipse.<sup id="cite_ref-SEpath_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEpath-12">[12]</a></sup> This is, however, not completely correct: the definition of a central eclipse is an eclipse during which the central line of the umbra touches the Earth's surface. It is possible, though extremely rare, that part of the umbra intersects with the Earth (thus creating an annular or total eclipse), but not its central line. This is then called a non-central total or annular eclipse.<sup id="cite_ref-SEpath_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEpath-12">[12]</a></sup> The last (umbral yet) non-central solar eclipse was <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_April_29,_2014" title="Solar eclipse of April 29, 2014">on April 29, 2014</a>. This was an annular eclipse. The next non-central total solar eclipse will be <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_April_9,_2043" title="Solar eclipse of April 9, 2043">on April 9, 2043</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg/220px-Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg/330px-Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg/440px-Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1623" data-file-height="2047" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exit_Diamond_Ring_Effect.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diamond Ring effect at third contact, marking the end of totality. Some prominences can also be seen.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The phases observed during a total eclipse are called:<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>First contact—when the Moon's limb (edge) is exactly tangential to the Sun's limb.</li>
<li>Second contact—starting with <a href="/wiki/Baily%27s_Beads" class="mw-redirect" title="Baily's Beads">Baily's Beads</a> (caused by light shining through valleys on the Moon's surface) and the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_ring_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond ring effect">diamond ring effect</a>. Almost the entire disk is covered.</li>
<li>Totality—the Moon obscures the entire disk of the Sun and only the solar corona is visible.</li>
<li>Third contact—when the first bright light becomes visible and the Moon's shadow is moving away from the observer. Again a diamond ring may be observed.</li>
<li>Fourth contact—when the trailing edge of the Moon ceases to overlap with the solar disk and the eclipse ends.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions">Predictions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry">Geometry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg/300px-Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg.png" width="300" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg/450px-Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg/600px-Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="895" data-file-height="680" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Geometry of a total solar eclipse (not to scale)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The diagrams to the right show the alignment of the Sun, Moon, and Earth during a solar eclipse. The dark gray region between the Moon and Earth is the <a href="/wiki/Umbra" class="mw-redirect" title="Umbra">umbra</a>, where the Sun is completely obscured by the Moon. The small area where the umbra touches Earth's surface is where a total eclipse can be seen. The larger light gray area is the <a href="/wiki/Penumbra" class="mw-redirect" title="Penumbra">penumbra</a>, in which a partial eclipse can be seen. An observer in the <a href="/wiki/Umbra#Antumbra" class="mw-redirect" title="Umbra">antumbra</a>, the area of shadow beyond the umbra, will see an annular eclipse.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Orbit_of_the_Moon" title="Orbit of the Moon">Moon's orbit</a> around the Earth is inclined at an angle of just over 5 degrees to the plane of the Earth's orbit around the Sun (the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>). Because of this, at the time of a new moon, the Moon will usually pass to the north or south of the Sun. A solar eclipse can occur only when new moon occurs close to one of the points (known as <a href="/wiki/Orbital_node" title="Orbital node">nodes</a>) where the Moon's orbit crosses the ecliptic.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>As noted above, the Moon's orbit is also <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">elliptical</a>. The Moon's distance from the Earth can vary by about 6% from its average value. Therefore, the Moon's apparent size varies with its distance from the Earth, and it is this effect that leads to the difference between total and annular eclipses. The distance of the Earth from the Sun also varies during the year, but this is a smaller effect. On average, the Moon appears to be slightly smaller than the Sun as seen from the Earth, so the majority (about 60%) of central eclipses are annular. It is only when the Moon is closer to the Earth than average (near its <a href="/wiki/Perigee" class="mw-redirect" title="Perigee">perigee</a>) that a total eclipse occurs.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>

<p>The Moon orbits the Earth in approximately 27.3 days, relative to a <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">fixed frame of reference</a>. This is known as the <a href="/wiki/Sidereal_month" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidereal month">sidereal month</a>. However, during one sidereal month, Earth has revolved part way around the Sun, making the average time between one new moon and the next longer than the sidereal month: it is approximately 29.5 days. This is known as the <a href="/wiki/Synodic_month" class="mw-redirect" title="Synodic month">synodic month</a> and corresponds to what is commonly called the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_month" title="Lunar month">lunar month</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Moon crosses from south to north of the ecliptic at its <a href="/wiki/Ascending_node" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascending node">ascending node</a>, and vice versa at its descending node.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington-16">[16]</a></sup> However, the nodes of the Moon's orbit are gradually moving in a <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde motion</a>, due to the action of the Sun's gravity on the Moon's motion, and they make a complete circuit every 18.6 years. This regression means that the time between each passage of the Moon through the ascending node is slightly shorter than the sidereal month. This period is called the nodical or <a href="/wiki/Draconic_month" class="mw-redirect" title="Draconic month">draconic month</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Finally, the Moon's perigee is moving forwards or precessing in its orbit and makes a complete circuit in 8.85 years. The time between one perigee and the next is slightly longer than the sidereal month and known as the <a href="/wiki/Anomalistic_month" class="mw-redirect" title="Anomalistic month">anomalistic month</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Moon's orbit intersects with the ecliptic at the two nodes that are 180 degrees apart. Therefore, the new moon occurs close to the nodes at two periods of the year approximately six months (173.3 days) apart, known as <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_season" title="Eclipse season">eclipse seasons</a>, and there will always be at least one solar eclipse during these periods. Sometimes the new moon occurs close enough to a node during two consecutive months to eclipse the Sun on both occasions in two partial eclipses. This means that, in any given year, there will always be at least two solar eclipses, and there can be as many as five.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eclipses can occur only when the Sun is within about 15 to 18 degrees of a node, (10 to 12 degrees for central eclipses). This is referred to as an eclipse limit. In the time it takes for the Moon to return to a node (draconic month), the apparent position of the Sun has moved about 29 degrees, relative to the nodes.<sup id="cite_ref-totality_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totality-1">[1]</a></sup> Since the eclipse limit creates a window of opportunity of up to 36 degrees (24 degrees for central eclipses), it is possible for partial eclipses (or rarely a partial and a central eclipse) to occur in consecutive months.<sup id="cite_ref-period_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-period-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg/220px-Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg/330px-Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg/440px-Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Graph_solar_eclipse_coverage.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fraction of the Sun's disc covered, <i>f</i>, when the same-sized discs are offset a fraction <i>t</i> of their diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Path">Path</span></h3>
<p>During a central eclipse, the Moon's umbra (or antumbra, in the case of an annular eclipse) moves rapidly from west to east across the Earth. The Earth is also rotating from west to east, at about 28&#160;km/min at the Equator, but as the Moon is moving in the same direction as the Earth's spin at about 61&#160;km/min, the umbra almost always appears to move in a roughly west-east direction across a map of the Earth at the speed of the Moon's orbital velocity minus the Earth's rotational velocity.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The width of the track of a central eclipse varies according to the relative apparent diameters of the Sun and Moon. In the most favourable circumstances, when a total eclipse occurs very close to perigee, the track can be up to 267&#160;km (166&#160;mi) wide and the duration of totality may be over 7 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Outside of the central track, a partial eclipse is seen over a much larger area of the Earth. Typically, the umbra is 100–160&#160;km wide, while the penumbral diameter is in excess of 6400&#160;km.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Duration">Duration</span></h3>
<p>The following factors determine the duration of a total solar eclipse (in order of decreasing importance):<sup id="cite_ref-longest_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longest-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>The moon being almost exactly at perigee (making its angular diameter as large as possible).</li>
<li>The earth being very near <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">aphelion</a> (furthest away from the sun in its elliptical orbit, making its angular diameter nearly as small as possible).</li>
<li>The midpoint of the eclipse being very close to the earth's equator, where the rotational velocity is greatest.</li>
<li>The vector of the eclipse path at the midpoint of the eclipse aligning with the vector of the earth's rotation (i.e. not diagonal but due east).</li>
<li>The midpoint of the eclipse being near the <a href="/wiki/Subsolar_point" title="Subsolar point">subsolar point</a> (the part of the earth closest to the sun).</li>
</ol>
<p>The longest eclipse that has been calculated thus far is the eclipse of <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_16,_2186" title="Solar eclipse of July 16, 2186">July 16, 2186</a> (with a maximum duration of 7 minutes 4 seconds over northern Guyana).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_cycles">Occurrence and cycles</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_cycle" title="Eclipse cycle">Eclipse cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif/300px-Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif" width="300" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif/450px-Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif/600px-Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif 2x" data-file-width="1930" data-file-height="1453" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Total_Solar_Eclipse_Paths-_1001-2000.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Total solar eclipse paths: 1001–2000, showing that total solar eclipses occur almost everywhere on Earth. This image was merged from 50 separate images from <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Total solar eclipses are rare events. Although they occur somewhere on Earth every 18 months on average,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> it is estimated that they recur at any given place only once every 360 to 410 years, on average.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The total eclipse lasts for only a maximum of a few minutes at any location, because the Moon's umbra moves eastward at over 1700&#160;km/h.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Totality currently can never last more than 7&#160;min 32&#160;s. This value changes over the millennia and is currently decreasing. By the 8th millennium, the longest theoretically possible total eclipse will be less than 7&#160;min 2&#160;s.<sup id="cite_ref-longest_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longest-29">[29]</a></sup> The last time an eclipse longer than 7 minutes occurred was <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_June_30,_1973" title="Solar eclipse of June 30, 1973">June 30, 1973</a> (7 min 3 sec). Observers aboard a <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Concorde</a> supersonic aircraft were able to stretch totality for this eclipse to about 74 minutes by flying along the path of the Moon's umbra.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The next total eclipse exceeding seven minutes in duration will not occur until <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_June_25,_2150" title="Solar eclipse of June 25, 2150">June 25, 2150</a>. The longest total solar eclipse during the 11,000 year period from 3000 BC to at least 8000 AD will occur on <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_16,_2186" title="Solar eclipse of July 16, 2186">July 16, 2186</a>, when totality will last 7&#160;min 29&#160;s.<sup id="cite_ref-longest_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longest-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> For comparison, the longest total eclipse of the 20th century at 7&#160;min 8&#160;s occurred on <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_June_20,_1955" title="Solar eclipse of June 20, 1955">June 20, 1955</a>, and there are no total solar eclipses over 7&#160;min in duration in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the date and time of any solar eclipse are known, it is possible to predict other eclipses using <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_cycle" title="Eclipse cycle">eclipse cycles</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Saros_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Saros cycle">saros</a> is probably the best known and one of the most accurate. A saros lasts 6,585.3 days (a little over 18 years), which means that, after this period, a practically identical eclipse will occur. The most notable difference will be a westward shift of about 120° in longitude (due to the 0.3 days) and a little in latitude (north-south for odd-numbered cycles, the reverse for even-numbered ones). A saros series always starts with a partial eclipse near one of Earth's polar regions, then shifts over the globe through a series of annular or total eclipses, and ends with a partial eclipse at the opposite polar region. A saros series lasts 1226 to 1550 years and 69 to 87 eclipses, with about 40 to 60 of them being central.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequency_per_year">Frequency per year</span></h3>
<p>Between two and five solar eclipses occur every year, with at least one per <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_season" title="Eclipse season">eclipse season</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> was instituted in 1582, years that have had five solar eclipses were 1693, 1758, 1805, 1823, 1870, and 1935. The next occurrence will be 2206.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> On average, there are about 240 solar eclipses each century.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
