<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_totality">Final totality</span></h3>
<p>Total solar eclipses are seen on Earth because of a fortuitous combination of circumstances. Even on Earth, the diversity of eclipses familiar to people today is a temporary (on a geological time scale) phenomenon. Hundreds of millions of years in the past, the Moon was closer to the Earth and therefore apparently larger, so every solar eclipse was total or partial, and there were no annular eclipses. Due to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">tidal acceleration</a>, the orbit of the Moon around the Earth becomes approximately 3.8&#160;cm more distant each year. Millions of years in the future, the Moon will be too far away to fully occlude the Sun, and no total eclipses will occur. In the same timeframe, the Sun may become brighter, making it appear larger in size.<sup id="cite_ref-fourmilab_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fourmilab-41">[41]</a></sup> Estimates of the when the moon will be unable to occlude the entire sun when viewed from the Earth range between 1.4 billion<sup id="cite_ref-fourmilab_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fourmilab-41">[41]</a></sup> and 650 million years in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_eclipses">Historical eclipses</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg/170px-Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg/255px-Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg/340px-Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="398" data-file-height="514" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine_Caron_Astronomers_Studying_an_Eclipse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Astronomers Studying an Eclipse</i> painted by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Caron" title="Antoine Caron">Antoine Caron</a> in 1571</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historical eclipses are a very valuable resource for historians, in that they allow a few historical events to be dated precisely, from which other dates and ancient calendars may be deduced. A <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_eclipse" title="Assyrian eclipse">solar eclipse of June 15, 763 BC</a> mentioned in an <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> text is important for the <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_ancient_Near_East" title="Chronology of the ancient Near East">chronology of the ancient Near East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> There have been other claims to date earlier eclipses. The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Joshua" title="Book of Joshua">Book of Joshua</a> 10:13 could refer to an annular eclipse on 30 October 1207 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The King <a href="/wiki/Zhong_Kang" title="Zhong Kang">Zhong Kang</a> supposedly beheaded two astronomers, Hsi and Ho, who failed to predict an eclipse 4,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Perhaps the earliest still-unproven claim is that of archaeologist Bruce Masse, who putatively links an eclipse that occurred on May 10, 2807 BC with a possible <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">meteor impact</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> on the basis of several ancient <a href="/wiki/Flood_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flood (mythology)">flood myths</a> that mention a total solar eclipse.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eclipses have been interpreted as <a href="/wiki/Omen" title="Omen">omens</a>, or portents.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The ancient Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> wrote that <a href="/wiki/Thales" class="mw-redirect" title="Thales">Thales of Miletus</a> predicted <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_of_Thales" title="Eclipse of Thales">an eclipse that occurred during a battle</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydians</a>. Both sides put down their weapons and declared peace as a result of the eclipse.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The exact eclipse involved remains uncertain, although the issue has been studied by hundreds of ancient and modern authorities. One likely candidate took place on May 28, 585 BC, probably near the <a href="/wiki/Halys_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Halys River">Halys</a> river in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> An eclipse recorded by Herodotus before <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes</a> departed for his expedition against <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> which is traditionally dated to 480 BC, was matched by <a href="/wiki/John_Russell_Hind" title="John Russell Hind">John Russell Hind</a> to an annular eclipse of the Sun at <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> on February 17, 478 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Alternatively, a partial eclipse was visible from Persia on October 2, 480 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Espenak_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Espenak-52">[52]</a></sup> Herodotus also reports a solar eclipse at <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The date of the eclipse (August 1, 477 BC) does not match exactly the conventional dates for the invasion accepted by historians.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chinese records of eclipses begin at around 720 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-SciAm_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-55">[55]</a></sup> The 4th century BC astronomer <a href="/wiki/Shi_Shen" title="Shi Shen">Shi Shen</a> described the prediction of eclipses by using the relative positions of the Moon and Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-Needham411_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Needham411-56">[56]</a></sup> The "radiating influence" theory (i.e., the Moon's light was reflection from the Sun) was existent in Chinese thought from about the sixth century BC (in the <i>Zhi Ran</i> of Zhi Ni Zi),<sup id="cite_ref-Needham227_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Needham227-57">[57]</a></sup> though it was opposed by the 1st century AD philosopher <a href="/wiki/Wang_Chong" title="Wang Chong">Wang Chong</a>, who made clear in his writing that this theory was nothing new.<sup id="cite_ref-Needham411_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Needham411-56">[56]</a></sup> Ancient Greeks, such as <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, also supported the theory of the Moon shining because of reflected light.<sup id="cite_ref-Needham227_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Needham227-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Attempts have been made to establish the exact date of <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday" title="Good Friday">Good Friday</a> by assuming that the <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_darkness_and_eclipse" class="mw-redirect" title="Crucifixion darkness and eclipse">darkness described at Jesus's crucifixion</a> was a solar eclipse. This research has not yielded conclusive results,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and Good Friday is recorded as being at <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Passover</a>, which is held at the time of a full moon. Further, the darkness lasted from the sixth hour to the ninth, or three hours, which is much, much longer than the eight-minute upper limit for any solar eclipse's totality. In the Western hemisphere, there are few reliable records of eclipses before 800 AD, until the advent of Arab and monastic observations in the early medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-SciAm_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-55">[55]</a></sup> The first recorded observation of the corona was made in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in 968 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-Espenak_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Espenak-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SciAm_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first known telescopic observation of a total solar eclipse was made in France in 1706.<sup id="cite_ref-SciAm_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-55">[55]</a></sup> Nine years later, English astronomer <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Halley" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund Halley">Edmund Halley</a> accurately predicted and observed the <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_3,_1715" title="Solar eclipse of May 3, 1715">solar eclipse of May 3, 1715</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Espenak_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Espenak-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SciAm_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-55">[55]</a></sup> By the mid-19th century, scientific understanding of the Sun was improving through observations of the Sun's corona during solar eclipses. The corona was identified as part of the Sun's atmosphere in <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_8,_1842" title="Solar eclipse of July 8, 1842">1842</a>, and the first photograph (or <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a>) of a total eclipse was taken of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_28,_1851" title="Solar eclipse of July 28, 1851">solar eclipse of July 28, 1851</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Espenak_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Espenak-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spectroscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Spectroscope">Spectroscope</a> observations were made of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_August_18,_1868" title="Solar eclipse of August 18, 1868">solar eclipse of August 18, 1868</a>, which helped to determine the chemical composition of the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-Espenak_52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Espenak-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia,_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia%2C_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg/220px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia%2C_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia%2C_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg/330px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia%2C_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia%2C_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg/440px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia%2C_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2039" data-file-height="1557" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acta_Eruditorum_-_I_astronomia,_1762_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13450778.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration from <i>De magna eclipsi solari, quae continget anno 1764</i> published in <i>Acta Eruditorum</i>, 1762</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Fiske_(philosopher)" title="John Fiske (philosopher)">John Fiske</a> summed up myths about the solar eclipse like this in his 1872 book <i>Myth and Myth-Makers</i>,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>the myth of Hercules and Cacus, the fundamental idea is the victory of the solar god over the robber who steals the light. Now whether the robber carries off the light in the evening when Indra has gone to sleep, or boldly rears his black form against the sky during the daytime, causing darkness to spread over the earth, would make little difference to the framers of the myth. To a chicken a solar eclipse is the same thing as nightfall, and he goes to roost accordingly. Why, then, should the primitive thinker have made a distinction between the darkening of the sky caused by black clouds and that caused by the rotation of the earth? He had no more conception of the scientific explanation of these phenomena than the chicken has of the scientific explanation of an eclipse. For him it was enough to know that the solar radiance was stolen, in the one case as in the other, and to suspect that the same demon was to blame for both robberies.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Viewing">Viewing</span></h2>
<p>Looking directly at the <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">photosphere</a> of the Sun (the bright disk of the Sun itself), even for just a few seconds, can cause permanent <a href="/wiki/Solar_retinopathy" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar retinopathy">damage</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> of the eye, because of the intense visible and invisible radiation that the photosphere emits. This damage can result in impairment of vision, up to and including <a href="/wiki/Blindness" class="mw-redirect" title="Blindness">blindness</a>. The retina has no sensitivity to pain, and the effects of retinal damage may not appear for hours, so there is no warning that injury is occurring.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under normal conditions, the Sun is so bright that it is difficult to stare at it directly. However, during an eclipse, with so much of the Sun covered, it is easier and more tempting to stare at it. Looking at the Sun during an eclipse is as dangerous as looking at it outside an eclipse, except during the brief period of totality, when the Sun's disk is completely covered (totality occurs only during a total eclipse and only very briefly; it does not occur during a partial or annular eclipse). Viewing the Sun's disk through any kind of optical aid (binoculars, a telescope, or even an optical camera viewfinder) is extremely hazardous and can cause irreversible eye damage within a fraction of a second.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Partial_and_annular_eclipses">Partial and annular eclipses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eclipsbrilletje.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Eclipsbrilletje.JPG/220px-Eclipsbrilletje.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Eclipsbrilletje.JPG/330px-Eclipsbrilletje.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Eclipsbrilletje.JPG/440px-Eclipsbrilletje.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eclipsbrilletje.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Solar_viewer" title="Solar viewer">Eclipse glasses</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg/220px-Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg/330px-Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg/440px-Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2190" data-file-height="1356" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_in_Turkey_March_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pinhole projection method of observing partial solar eclipse. Insert (upper left): partially eclipsed Sun photographed with a white solar filter. Main image: projections of the partially eclipsed Sun (bottom right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Viewing the Sun during partial and annular eclipses (and during total eclipses outside the brief period of totality) requires special eye protection, or indirect viewing methods if eye damage is to be avoided. The Sun's disk can be viewed using appropriate filtration to block the harmful part of the Sun's radiation. Sunglasses do not make viewing the Sun safe. Only properly designed and certified solar filters should be used for direct viewing of the Sun's disk.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Especially, self-made filters using common objects such as a <a href="/wiki/Floppy_disk" title="Floppy disk">floppy disk</a> removed from its case, a <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Compact Disc">Compact Disc</a>, a black colour slide film, smoked glass, etc. must be avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-perkins_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perkins-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The safest way to view the Sun's disk is by indirect projection.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington25_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington25-68">[68]</a></sup> This can be done by projecting an image of the disk onto a white piece of paper or card using a pair of binoculars (with one of the lenses covered), a telescope, or another piece of cardboard with a small hole in it (about 1&#160;mm diameter), often called a <a href="/wiki/Pinhole_camera" title="Pinhole camera">pinhole camera</a>. The projected image of the Sun can then be safely viewed; this technique can be used to observe <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a>, as well as eclipses. Care must be taken, however, to ensure that no one looks through the projector (telescope, pinhole, etc.) directly.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Viewing the Sun's disk on a video display screen (provided by a <a href="/wiki/Video_camera" title="Video camera">video camera</a> or <a href="/wiki/Digital_camera" title="Digital camera">digital camera</a>) is safe, although the camera itself may be damaged by direct exposure to the Sun. The optical viewfinders provided with some video and digital cameras are not safe. Securely mounting #14 welder's glass in front of the lens and viewfinder protects the equipment and makes viewing possible.<sup id="cite_ref-perkins_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perkins-67">[67]</a></sup> Professional workmanship is essential because of the dire consequences any gaps or detaching mountings will have. In the partial eclipse path, one will not be able to see the corona or nearly complete darkening of the sky. However, depending on how much of the Sun's disk is obscured, some darkening may be noticeable. If three-quarters or more of the sun is obscured, then an effect can be observed by which the daylight appears to be dim, as if the sky were overcast, yet objects still cast sharp shadows.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Totality">Totality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg/120px-August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg" width="120" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg/180px-August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg/240px-August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="673" data-file-height="776" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:August_21_2017_solar_eclipse_baily_beads_TLR2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Baily%27s_beads" title="Baily's beads">Baily's beads</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When the shrinking visible part of the photosphere becomes very small, <a href="/wiki/Baily%27s_beads" title="Baily's beads">Baily's beads</a> will occur. These are caused by the sunlight still being able to reach the Earth through lunar valleys. Totality then begins with the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_ring_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond ring effect">diamond ring effect</a>, the last bright flash of sunlight.<sup id="cite_ref-total_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-total-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is safe to observe the total phase of a solar eclipse directly only when the Sun's photosphere is completely covered by the Moon, and not before or after totality.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrington25_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington25-68">[68]</a></sup> During this period, the Sun is too dim to be seen through filters. The Sun's faint <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> will be visible, and the <a href="/wiki/Chromosphere" title="Chromosphere">chromosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_prominence" title="Solar prominence">solar prominences</a>, and possibly even a <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flare</a> may be seen. At the end of totality, the same effects will occur in reverse order, and on the opposite side of the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-total_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-total-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photography">Photography</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg/270px-2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg" width="270" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg/405px-2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg/540px-2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5104" data-file-height="2904" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The progression of a <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_August_1,_2008" title="Solar eclipse of August 1, 2008">solar eclipse on August 1, 2008</a> in <a href="/wiki/Novosibirsk" title="Novosibirsk">Novosibirsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. All times UTC (local time was UTC+7). The time span between shots is three minutes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Photographing an eclipse is possible with fairly common camera equipment. In order for the disk of the Sun/Moon to be easily visible, a fairly high magnification <a href="/wiki/Long_focus_lens" class="mw-redirect" title="Long focus lens">long focus lens</a> is needed (at least 200&#160;mm for a 35&#160;mm camera), and for the disk to fill most of the frame, a longer lens is needed (over 500&#160;mm). As with viewing the Sun directly, looking at it through the optical viewfinder of a camera can produce damage to the retina, so care is recommended.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Solar filters are required for digital photography even if an optical viewfinder is not used. Using a camera's live view feature or an electronic viewfinder is safe for the human eye, but the Sun's rays could potentially irreparably damage digital image sensors unless the lens is covered by a properly designed solar filter.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eclipse_chasing">Eclipse chasing</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_chasing" title="Eclipse chasing">Eclipse chasing</a></div>
<p>A decidated group of eclipse chasers have pursued the observation of solar eclipses when they occur around the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> A person who chases eclipses is known as an umbraphile, meaning shadow lover.<sup id="cite_ref-sola_Umbr_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sola_Umbr-75">[75]</a></sup> Umbraphiles travel for eclipses and use various tools to help view the sun including <a href="/wiki/Solar_viewer" title="Solar viewer">solar viewing glasses</a>, also known as eclipse glasses, as well as telescopes.<sup id="cite_ref-ecli_Safe_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecli_Safe-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-atla_Chas_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atla_Chas-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_observations">Other observations</span></h2>
<p>A total solar eclipse provides a rare opportunity to observe the <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> (the outer layer of the Sun's atmosphere). Normally this is not visible because the <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">photosphere</a> is much brighter than the corona. According to the point reached in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_cycle" title="Solar cycle">solar cycle</a>, the corona may appear small and symmetric, or large and fuzzy. It is very hard to predict this in advance.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the light filters through leaves of trees during a partial eclipse, the overlapping leaves create natural pinholes, displaying mini eclipses on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Phenomena associated with eclipses include <a href="/wiki/Shadow_bands" title="Shadow bands">shadow bands</a> (also known as <i>flying shadows</i>), which are similar to shadows on the bottom of a swimming pool. They only occur just prior to and after totality, when a narrow solar crescent acts as an <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropic</a> light source.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1919_observations">1919 observations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tests_of_general_relativity#Deflection_of_light_by_the_Sun" title="Tests of general relativity">Tests of general relativity §&#160;Deflection of light by the Sun</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1919_eclipse_negative.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1919_eclipse_negative.jpg/170px-1919_eclipse_negative.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1919_eclipse_negative.jpg/255px-1919_eclipse_negative.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1919_eclipse_negative.jpg/340px-1919_eclipse_negative.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="899" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1919_eclipse_negative.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eddington's original photograph of the 1919 eclipse, which provided evidence for <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The observation of a total <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_29,_1919" title="Solar eclipse of May 29, 1919">solar eclipse of May 29, 1919</a>, helped to confirm <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>. By comparing the apparent distance between stars in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">Taurus</a>, with and without the Sun between them, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> stated that the <a href="/wiki/Predictive_power" title="Predictive power">theoretical predictions</a> about <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lenses</a> were confirmed.<sup id="cite_ref-Eddington1920_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eddington1920-81">[81]</a></sup> The observation with the Sun between the stars was only possible during totality since the stars are then visible. Though Eddington's observations were near the experimental limits of accuracy at the time, work in the later half of the 20th century confirmed his results.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravity_anomalies">Gravity anomalies</span></h3>
<p>There is a long history of observations of gravity-related phenomena during solar eclipses, especially during the period of totality. In 1954, and again in 1959, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Allais" title="Maurice Allais">Maurice Allais</a> reported observations of strange and unexplained movement during solar eclipses.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The reality of this phenomenon, named the <a href="/wiki/Allais_effect" title="Allais effect">Allais effect</a>, has remained controversial. Similarly, in 1970, <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Saxl" title="Erwin Saxl">Saxl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mildred_Allen" title="Mildred Allen">Allen</a> observed the sudden change in motion of a torsion pendulum; this phenomenon is called the Saxl effect.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>A recent published observation during the 1997 solar eclipse by Wang <i>et al.</i> suggested a possible <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_shielding" title="Gravitational shielding">gravitational shielding</a> effect,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> which generated debate. Later in 2002, Yang and Wang published detailed data analysis, which suggested that the phenomenon still remains unexplained.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eclipses_and_transits">Eclipses and transits</span></h3>
<p>In principle, the simultaneous occurrence of a Solar eclipse and a <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomical transit">transit</a> of a planet is possible. But these events are extremely rare because of their short durations. The next anticipated simultaneous occurrence of a Solar eclipse and a <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Mercury" title="Transit of Mercury">transit of Mercury</a> will be on July 5, 6757, and a Solar eclipse and a <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">transit of Venus</a> is expected on April 5, 15232.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>More common, but still infrequent, is a <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_conjunction" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomical conjunction">conjunction</a> of a planet (especially, but not only, Mercury or Venus) at the time of a total solar eclipse, in which event the planet will be visible very near the eclipsed Sun, when without the eclipse it would have been lost in the Sun's glare. At one time, some scientists hypothesized that there may be a planet (often given the name <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(hypothetical_planet)" title="Vulcan (hypothetical planet)">Vulcan</a>) even closer to the Sun than Mercury; the only way to confirm its existence would have been to observe it in transit or during a total solar eclipse. No such planet was ever found, and <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> has since explained the observations that led astronomers to suggest that Vulcan might exist.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthshine">Earthshine</span></h3>
<p>During a total solar eclipse, the Moon's shadow covers only a small fraction of the Earth. The Earth continues to receive at least 92 percent of the amount of sunlight it receives without an eclipse&#160;–&#160;more if the <a href="/wiki/Penumbra" class="mw-redirect" title="Penumbra">penumbra</a> of the Moon's shadow partly misses the Earth. Seen from the Moon, the Earth during a total solar eclipse is mostly brilliantly illuminated, with only a small dark patch showing the Moon's shadow. The brilliantly-lit Earth reflects a lot of light to the Moon. If the <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> of the eclipsed Sun were not present, the Moon, illuminated by earthlight, would be easily visible from Earth. This would be essentially the same as the <a href="/wiki/Earthshine" class="mw-redirect" title="Earthshine">earthshine</a> which can frequently be seen when the Moon's <a href="/wiki/Lunar_phase" title="Lunar phase">phase</a> is a narrow crescent. In reality, the corona, though much less brilliant than the Sun's <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">photosphere</a>, is much brighter than the Moon illuminated by earthlight. Therefore, by contrast, the Moon during a total solar eclipse appears to be black, with the corona surrounding it.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_satellites">Artificial satellites</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif/220px-An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif/330px-An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif/440px-An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:An_EPIC_Eclipse.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From space, the Moon's shadow during a solar eclipse appears as a dark spot moving across the Earth.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg/220px-Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg/330px-Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg/440px-Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3040" data-file-height="2016" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eclipse_fromISS_2006-03-29.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Moon's shadow over <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, seen from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a> during a <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_March_29,_2006" title="Solar eclipse of March 29, 2006">2006 total solar eclipse</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Artificial satellites can also pass in front of the Sun as seen from the Earth, but none is large enough to cause an eclipse. At the altitude of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, for example, an object would need to be about 3.35&#160;km (2.08&#160;mi) across to blot the Sun out entirely. These transits are difficult to watch because the zone of visibility is very small. The satellite passes over the face of the Sun in about a second, typically. As with a transit of a planet, it will not get dark.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Observations of eclipses from spacecraft or artificial satellites orbiting above the Earth's atmosphere are not subject to weather conditions. The crew of <a href="/wiki/Gemini_12" title="Gemini 12">Gemini 12</a> observed a total solar eclipse from space in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The partial phase of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_August_11,_1999" title="Solar eclipse of August 11, 1999">1999 total eclipse</a> was visible from <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Apollo%E2%80%93Soyuz_Test_Project" title="Apollo–Soyuz Test Project">Apollo–Soyuz Test Project</a> conducted in July 1975, the Apollo spacecraft was positioned to create an artificial solar eclipse giving the Soyuz crew an opportunity to photograph the <a href="/wiki/Solar_corona" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar corona">solar corona</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_March_20,_2015" title="Solar eclipse of March 20, 2015">solar eclipse of March 20, 2015</a>, was the first occurrence of an eclipse estimated to potentially have a significant impact on the power system, with the electricity sector taking measures to mitigate any impact. The <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_grid_of_Continental_Europe" title="Synchronous grid of Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Grid_(Great_Britain)" title="National Grid (Great Britain)">Great Britain</a> synchronous areas were estimated to have about 90 <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">gigawatts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> and it was estimated that production would temporarily decrease by up to 34 GW compared to a clear sky day.<sup id="cite_ref-eclImp_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eclImp-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The temperature may decrease by 3&#160;°C, and <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> potentially decreases as winds are reduced by 0.7&#160;m/s.<sup id="cite_ref-RSwind_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSwind-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the drop in light level and air temperature, animals change their behavior during totality. For example, birds and squirrels return to their nests and crickets chirp.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_and_forthcoming_solar_eclipses">Recent and forthcoming solar eclipses</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_solar_eclipses_in_the_21st_century" title="List of solar eclipses in the 21st century">List of solar eclipses in the 21st century</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_solar_eclipses" title="Lists of solar eclipses">Lists of solar eclipses</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png/300px-Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png" width="300" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png/450px-Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png/600px-Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png 2x" data-file-width="1942" data-file-height="1465" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Central_eclipses_2001-2020.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eclipse path for total and hybrid eclipses from 2001 to 2020.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eclipses only occur in the <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_season" title="Eclipse season">eclipse season</a>, when the Sun is close to either the ascending or descending <a href="/wiki/Lunar_node" title="Lunar node">node of the Moon</a>. Each eclipse is separated by one, five or six <a href="/wiki/Lunation" class="mw-redirect" title="Lunation">lunations</a> (<a href="/wiki/Synodic_month" class="mw-redirect" title="Synodic month">synodic months</a>), and the midpoint of each season is separated by 173.3 days, which is the mean time for the Sun to travel from one node to the next. The period is a little less than half a calendar year because the lunar nodes slowly regress. Because 223 synodic months is roughly equal to 239 <a href="/wiki/Anomalistic_month" class="mw-redirect" title="Anomalistic month">anomalistic months</a> and 242 <a href="/wiki/Draconic_month" class="mw-redirect" title="Draconic month">draconic months</a>, eclipses with similar geometry recur 223 synodic months (about 6,585.3 days) apart. This period (18 years 11.3 days) is a <a href="/wiki/Saros_(astronomy)" title="Saros (astronomy)">saros</a>. Because 223 synodic months is not identical to 239 anomalistic months or 242 draconic months, saros cycles do not endlessly repeat. Each cycle begins with the Moon's shadow crossing the earth near the north or south pole, and subsequent events progress toward the other pole until the Moon's shadow misses the earth and the series ends.<sup id="cite_ref-period_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-period-23">[23]</a></sup> Saros cycles are numbered; currently, cycles 117 to 156 are active. The next solar eclipse will occur on <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_February_15,_2018" title="Solar eclipse of February 15, 2018">February 15, 2018</a>. It will be a partial solar eclipse visible from Antarctica and south South America.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://education.gsfc.nasa.gov/eclipse/pages/faq.html">NASA – Eclipse 99 – Frequently Asked Questions</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100527142627/http://education.gsfc.nasa.gov/eclipse/pages/faq.html">Archived</a> 2010-05-27 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. – There is a mistake in the <i>How long will we continue to be able to see total eclipses of the Sun?</i> answer, "...the Sun's angular diameter varies from 32.7 minutes of arc when the Earth is at its farthest point in its orbit (aphelion), and 31.6 arc minutes when it is at its closest (perihelion)." It should appear smaller when farther, so the values should be swapped.</span></li>
